> Implementation Roadmap: 30-60-90 and Beyond Implementation Roadmap: 30-60-90 and Beyond SEND ME THE RESOURCE Visit Umbrex data and analytics Practices A governance design may be sound on paper and still fail in execution if the implementation roadmap is too ambitious, too abstract, or too detached from operating reality. Many organizations know what “good” governance looks like in principle. Far fewer know how to stand it up in a way that produces visible progress in the first month, credible proof in the first quarter, and durable capability over several years. That sequencing matters. Move too slowly and sponsorship decays. Move too broadly and the business sees only new requirements. Move too tactically and the program produces local wins without a path to scale. 17.1 The First 30 Days: Foundation, Quick Wins, and Governance MVP The first thirty days of a governance program should not be used to produce a grand enterprise design that attempts to settle every policy, tooling, and organizational question. The objective of the first month is to create enough structure that the organization can stop improvising, start making decisions visibly, and begin demonstrating that governance will solve real problems rather than generate more meetings. This is the period in which the program establishes its minimum viable governance model, secures sponsor commitment, chooses where to start, and delivers a small number of quick wins that prove the program is grounded in business pain. The first task is sponsor alignment. Governance cannot survive as a data-office side project. Within the first two weeks, the executive sponsor or sponsor group should agree on the business case, the initial scope, the few outcomes that matter most, and the decision rights that will require visible backing from leadership. The program should not try to persuade executives with theory. It should use the diagnostic evidence gathered earlier to show where poor ownership, weak definitions, slow access, repeated quality defects, or unmanaged changes are hurting performance or raising risk. The sponsor discussion should end with concrete commitments: named pilot domains, named accountable owners, agreed success measures, and a willingness to intervene if ownership conflicts arise. The second task is scoping the governance MVP. In the first thirty days, “MVP” does not mean a stripped-down slide deck. It means a small but working operating model. That model should include the initial domains or use cases, named owners and stewards, a starter set of decisions, basic workflow paths for access, definitions, issues, and exceptions, and a short scorecard. The better path is to choose one or two domains where the pain is visible, the sponsor is credible, and the scope can be bounded. Quick wins matter in this phase because they shape perception. The organization needs to feel, quickly, that governance is making work easier or safer in specific places. Good quick wins are usually small operational improvements with visible business relevance. Examples include naming clear owners for a disputed metric set, simplifying an access approval path for a standard use case, publishing approved definitions for a few high-conflict terms, standing up a decision log so repeated debates stop cycling, or launching a structured issue register for a recurring quality problem. These wins demonstrate that governance is reducing ambiguity. The first month should also establish the core program rhythm. That means agreeing on a sponsor check-in cadence, setting up domain-level working sessions, creating an intake path for requests and issues, and assigning someone to maintain the decision log, action tracker, and scorecard. Governance often stalls because everyone assumes the operating machinery will somehow run itself once the design is announced. It will not. A named program lead, a stewarding mechanism, and a visible tracker for open decisions are more important than a perfect future-state architecture. Tooling in the first month should be deliberately light. The organization should use existing platforms where possible for intake, ticketing, document control, and evidence capture. The objective is not to postpone technology indefinitely, but to avoid spending the first month configuring a large platform before the core workflow is stable. Minimum viable tooling may be as simple as a shared request queue, a governed glossary workspace, a decision register, and a basic scorecard format. Baseline metrics should be captured before the first month ends. These should include current cycle times for a few important decisions, ownership coverage for the pilot scope, issue aging where relevant, and any visible business pain the pilot is intended to address. Without a baseline, the sixty-day and ninety-day reviews will drift into anecdote. A practical first-thirty-days checklist usually includes: Sponsorship: confirm executive sponsor, pilot domains, and success measures. Scope: define the MVP boundaries, key assets, and critical decisions. Roles: assign domain owners, stewards, custodians, and escalation points. Workflows: stand up minimum paths for definitions, access, issues, and exceptions. Quick wins: complete two to four improvements that remove visible friction. Metrics: capture the baseline and publish the first pilot scorecard draft. If the first thirty days go well, the organization should be able to answer a simple question credibly: where is governance now operating, who is accountable there, and what has already improved because of it. That is enough to justify moving into domain pilots with real confidence. 17.2 60 Days: Domain Pilots, Measurable Improvements, Tooling Minimums By day sixty, governance must move from foundational setup to observable operating results. This is the phase in which domain pilots become the proving ground for the model. The goal is not to reach enterprise scale yet. It is to show that in a bounded domain, the combination of ownership, standard workflows, metadata, controls, and scorecards produces better outcomes than the prior ad hoc approach. If the first thirty days created belief, the next thirty should create proof. Domain pilot selection is critical. A strong pilot domain has three characteristics. First, it matters enough that business leaders care about the outcome. Second, it is bound enough that the team can actually improve something within sixty days. Third, it has a sponsor and domain owner willing to make decisions quickly. A customer or product domain with unresolved ownership, recurring quality defects, and high analytical reuse may be a good candidate. A sprawling enterprise domain with dozens of systems, weak sponsorship, and major pending platform changes is usually a poor first pilot even if it is strategically important in the long run. The work in the pilot domain should be practical and visible. Owners should approve the initial set of critical data elements, domain terms, and quality thresholds. Stewards should begin running the glossary, issue log, exception register, and metadata maintenance for in-scope assets. Access workflows should move from informal approval toward standard routing for the pilot assets. Change notices should be tested on at least one material modification. In other words, the pilot should exercise the real governance muscles rather than only producing design artifacts. Measurable improvement is essential at this stage. The pilot does not need to transform the domain completely, but it should move a few metrics in ways that business users can understand. A shorter access cycle for standard requests, fewer unresolved severe issues, a reduction in disputed definitions, faster incident triage, or improved quality for a small set of critical elements are all strong pilot signals. The key is to choose measures the domain owner cares about and publish them regularly. Sixty days is also the point at which tooling minimums should become clearer. By now the team has seen where manual work is acceptable, where it is becoming a bottleneck, and which integrations would create immediate value. Some pilot programs will find that a catalog or glossary tool needs to be configured more formally. Others will see that access requests should move into an enterprise workflow platform, or that key quality rules need automated monitoring rather than spreadsheet tracking. The right response is selective enablement based on evidence from the pilot. A common mistake in the sixty-day phase is to treat the pilot as a special project run by exceptional people. That creates temporary success but weak learning. The better approach is to run the pilot using the same kinds of owners, stewards, forums, and tools the broader organization will later use. The pilot should test whether the intended operating model can work under realistic conditions. There should also be a formal review at the end of this phase. That review should not be an optimistic status presentation. It should ask hard questions. Which decisions became easier. Which workflows were still too slow. Which metadata fields were valuable and which were burdensome. Where did role ambiguity remain? Which control partners were appropriately engaged and which became bottlenecks. What automation is now justified. What would need to change before onboarding a second or third domain. The answers to those questions are the foundation for scaling. During this period, the program should begin publishing a simple pilot narrative to the wider organization. Not a victory lap, but a fact-based account of what the pilot changed and what is being learned. This helps create demand from adjacent domains while also signaling that governance is not being imposed blindly. It is being tested, refined, and proven. A practical sixty-day pilot package often includes: Domain governance pack: owners, stewards, scope, CDEs, scorecard, and active workflows. Pilot metrics: baseline versus current performance for a few high-value measures. Tooling needs: specific automation or integration requirements justified by pilot pain points. Lessons learned: what must change before scaling to additional domains. By the end of sixty days, the organization should be able to point to at least one domain in which governance is not theoretical. It is running, measured, and visibly better than the pre-pilot state. That evidence is what gives the central team permission to ask other domains to adopt the model with seriousness rather than curiosity. 17.3 90 Days: Scale to Additional Domains, Automate Controls, Embed Into SDLC The ninety-day mark is the first true inflection point. Up to this point, the program has been establishing credibility. Now it must prove that the model can scale without losing discipline or becoming a central bottleneck. This does not mean rolling governance out to every domain at once. It means taking the patterns that worked in the pilot, standardizing them enough to repeat, and embedding them into the delivery and change-management machinery of the organization so governance becomes part of how data work is done. The first priority is controlled expansion. A second and perhaps third domain should be onboarded using the refined pattern from the pilot. The onboarding should be faster than the original pilot because templates, decision paths, roles, and scorecards already exist. If every new domain still feels like a custom redesign, the model is not ready to scale. This is why the ninety-day phase should invest in repeatable assets: domain onboarding kits, role descriptions, workflow templates, change-control criteria, scorecard structures, and training materials tuned for new domain teams. At this stage, selective automation becomes more valuable. The pilot should have shown which controls are stable enough to automate and which still require human judgment. Standard access requests for known roles, metadata capture for required fields, quality monitoring for a handful of critical rules, alerting on failed freshness or schema checks, and exception-expiry reminders are often the first strong candidates. The point of automation is to reduce manual friction in the high-volume, repeatable parts of governance so humans can focus on judgment, escalation, and design improvement. Embedding governance into the software development life cycle, or SDLC, is one of the most important moves in this phase. If governance remains a separate review track, engineering teams will see it as extra work and release pressure will eventually push them around it. Governance expectations should therefore begin to appear in product onboarding, design review, pull-request templates, schema review, release checklists, and post-release monitoring. For a governed data asset, the SDLC should ask whether ownership is assigned, whether classification is confirmed, whether required metadata is present, whether critical quality checks are configured, whether change impact has been assessed, and whether consumers have been notified if the release is breaking or material. This is also the point at which the program should begin distinguishing between enterprise minimums and domain variation. The scaling model should define which elements are standardized everywhere and which can remain domain-specific. Ownership conventions, minimum metadata, change-notification rules for material releases, issue-severity definitions, and exception handling are usually good candidates for enterprise minimums. Quality rules, some local terminology, and domain backlog priorities can remain domain-specific. A ninety-day review should therefore consider not only pilot outcomes but also scale readiness. Can a new domain adopt the model with limited central support? Are the core roles understood? Are workflow tools adequate? Are engineering teams able to meet governance checks inside their normal process. Has the executive sponsor reinforced the model when trade-offs became difficult. Are metrics stable enough to compare across domains? The next phase depends less on design creativity and more on operational repeatability. The program should also guard against a subtle but common problem: early scale without adequate support. As governance shows value, more domains may ask to join. That demand is positive, but unmanaged demand can overwhelm the small central team and produce inconsistent implementations. A scaled roadmap should therefore include entry criteria for new domains, a simple readiness assessment, and a clear statement of what support the domain must bring itself. A practical ninety-day scaling agenda often includes: New domain onboarding: launch the next one or two domains using the standard pattern. Automation: implement the first repeatable controls and reminders. SDLC integration: add governance checks to design, change, and release routines. Standard assets: publish templates, training, and onboarding guides. Readiness rules: define what a domain must provide before joining the program. When ninety days are used well, governance stops looking like a promising pilot and starts looking like a scalable operating capability. That is the threshold the program must cross before it can justify multi-year investment with confidence. 17.4 Multi-Year Roadmap: Maturity Evolution and Funding Model After the first ninety days, governance enters a different planning horizon. The program is no longer proving that governance can work at all. It is deciding how quickly to expand, which capabilities to industrialize, and how to fund governance as part of the organization's ongoing operating model. This is the point at which many programs make a mistake in one direction or the other. Some remain permanently in pilot mode, adding domains slowly but never investing in the architecture, staffing, and automation needed for enterprise resilience. Others leap too quickly into a large transformation, purchasing extensive tools and declaring enterprise coverage before the operating model is mature enough to use them well. A multi-year roadmap should avoid both traps. The most practical way to think about multi-year maturity is in phases. The first phase is established: prove the model, stabilize governance routines, produce visible outcomes, and create reusable assets. The second phase is scale: extend governance to additional domains, increase the number of governed products and critical elements, improve cross-domain standards, and invest in meaningful automation and integration. The third phase is industrialization: deepen tooling integration, broaden control coverage, strengthen lineage and metadata for priority assets, and embed a mature service model. The fourth phase is optimize: refine service levels, reduce policy friction, retire weak controls, and align more fully with analytics, AI, and enterprise risk management. Each phase should have explicit maturity outcomes. Expansion alone is not maturity. A program can govern more domains and still operate poorly. The roadmap should therefore specify what changes in operating quality as the program matures. In the scale phase, the organization may expect most priority domains to have named owners, working scorecards, standard access paths, and active issue management. In the industrialized phase, it may expect automated control coverage for the most common workflows, integrated catalog and lineage for key products, and governance checks embedded in the SDLC for all material releases. In the optimize phase, it may expect proactive monitoring of drift and breakage, better self-service for low-risk requests, and consistent evidence for audit across the enterprise. Funding must evolve with this roadmap. In the first year, governance is often funded as a program, supported by a central team and a small set of pilot or sponsor-backed domain resources. That is appropriate early on, but it cannot sustain a broad scale forever. Over time, the funding model should reflect the fact that governance is both a shared enterprise capability and a domain operating responsibility. The central function usually funds methods, policy coordination, enterprise reporting, tooling administration, training, and selected shared services. Domains should increasingly fund or assign their own owner time, steward capacity, and technical implementation effort for domain-specific priorities. A practical multi-year funding model therefore blends central and federated investment. Central funding: enterprise standards, governance program leadership, shared tools, common training, cross-domain reporting, and enterprise controls. Domain funding: stewardship effort, local backlog execution, product-specific quality remediation, domain metadata maintenance, and change implementation inside the domain. Platform funding: automation, integration, observability, access-engineering improvements, and shared services that benefit multiple domains. The business case for ongoing funding should also mature. Early funding is justified by pain relief and risk reduction in pilot areas. Later funding should be justified by broader enterprise efficiency, reduced incident cost, improved delivery speed, and better support for platform modernization and AI use. The funding narrative should move from “we need to start governance” to “we need to scale the operating capability that is already proving its value.” Roadmaps should also include explicit renewal points. At least annually, leadership should review which domains are in scope, which capabilities still create friction, which controls can be simplified, what the next wave of automation should be, and whether the funding split remains appropriate. Mature programs evolve deliberately as the business, platform, and regulatory environment change. A practical multi-year roadmap summary might state: Year 1: establish the operating model, prove value in pilot domains, and launch limited automation. Year 2: scale to priority domains, standardize enterprise minimums, and embed governance in delivery workflows. Year 3: industrialize tooling integration, evidence capture, and control coverage for critical assets. Year 4 and beyond: optimize service levels, simplify where possible, and align governance with evolving analytics, AI, and regulatory demands. The roadmap should remain practical, but it should also remain ambitious. Governance is not a one-time project with a clean end date. It is an operating capability that should become more embedded, more automated, and more business-aligned over time. The first ninety days matter because they create credibility. The multi-year roadmap matters because credibility without scale is only a successful pilot. ◀ Previous Chapter Next Chapter ▶ ☰ Table of Contents ☰ Table of Contents Table of Contents Chapter 1. Import – Bring C4 Diagrams into Nodinite from Mermaid, PlantUML ...Source: https://docs.nodinite.com/Documentation/RepositoryModel?doc=/C4%20Diagrams/Features/Import
Import – Bring C4 Diagrams into Nodinite from Mermaid, PlantUML, Structurizr DSL, or Draw.io XML | Nodinite Docs × Explore Nodinite for 30 days – completely free Find out why this is the leading O&M software Prove your business case Test the self-service for the business side × See how Nodinite resolves your technical issues, in a LIVE Demo! 10 minutes to read Import Import brings existing C4 diagrams from Mermaid, PlantUML, Structurizr DSL, or Draw.io XML directly into your Nodinite Repository — mapping every node to the real entities that already live in your system. The import wizard resolves entities, handles conflicts, auto-creates cross-diagram navigation links within the Diagram Set, and asks you to confirm required metadata when the Draw.io source cannot provide it reliably. Tip Keep drill-through links current as your set grows. See Sync Diagram Links to rebuild navigation links across the full set at any time. Understanding the Import Architecture Import Two-Step Flow The import pipeline runs as two separate server operations. Both are required; neither alone produces a complete diagram. Paste Source\nMermaid / PlantUML / Structurizr DSL / Draw.io XML Validate\nParse + Match + Preview Review and Resolve\nConflict Resolution UI Apply\nCreate Diagram + Nodes + Boundaries + Connectors + Auto-Links Draft Diagram\nRepository-bound, live entity names Diagram: The import pipeline — validate parses your markup and matches Repository entities, review lets you resolve ambiguities, and apply creates the diagram record along with all cross-diagram navigation links. How Entity Matching Works During validation, each element parsed from your markup — every Container(...) , System(...) , Person(...) , and Boundary(...) — is compared against your Nodinite Repository: Important Boundaries resolve to Domains only. The import wizard never matches boundaries to Systems, regardless of the markup source. If your Integration Landscape data lives in the Nodinite Repository and you want a full Diagram Set generated from it — including correct System boundaries at L2 — use Generate from Integration Landscape instead. That pipeline derives boundaries from Repository entity IDs directly and does not go through this text import path. Matching uses both the alias (first positional argument in the C4 macro) and the display name (second positional argument). Both are tried before declaring a NameConflict or NotFound result. How Auto-Links Are Created Auto-links are cross-diagram navigation entries — the data that powers drill-through badges. They are created during Apply by comparing the newly imported diagram's Repository bindings against every other diagram in the same Diagram Set: For each match on the (RepositoryEntityType, RepositoryEntityId) pair, a cross-diagram link is created between the two diagrams Auto-link creation is add-only: existing links are never removed, only missing links are added Deduplication prevents the same link from being created twice within a single Apply run This means the drill-through badge on an L1 Context node pointing to an L2 Container diagram is stored as data in the cross-diagram link table — not embedded as text in any Mermaid markup. Supported Source Formats Mermaid C4 Mermaid is the native format. Paste standard Mermaid C4 markup with a top-level diagram type declaration on the first line. Supported diagram type declarations: C4Context C4Container C4Component C4Deployment C4Dynamic Supported macros: Supported directives: title — sets the diagram title UpdateLayoutConfig , UpdateRelStyle , UpdateElementStyle — recognized and skipped with a warning (not a parse error) Warning Unknown keyword-like macros after the diagram type declaration produce a parse error. Remove any custom macros not in the table above before importing. PlantUML C4 PlantUML C4 is accepted and normalized automatically before parsing: @startuml and @enduml tags are stripped !include and !pragma lines are removed The diagram type is inferred from !include names (e.g., !include <C4Context> → C4Context ) Common layout macros ( LAYOUT_WITH_LEGEND , LAYOUT_TOP_DOWN , etc.) are removed Mermaid-equivalent C4 keyword is prepended, then the standard Mermaid parser processes the result Standard C4-PlantUML macros ( Person , System , Container , Boundary , Rel ) work as-is after normalization. Non-C4 PlantUML constructs outside this normalization path may fail to parse. Structurizr DSL A C4-focused subset of Structurizr DSL is supported: workspace , model , and views blocks person and person_ext softwareSystem container Relationships declared as src -> tgt "Label" "Technology" view declarations — used to infer the diagram type Tip The Structurizr parser covers typical system and container model definitions. Custom DSL constructs specific to Structurizr, including themes, documentation embeds, and styling, are ignored or left uninterpreted. Draw.io XML Draw.io XML from draw.io / diagrams.net is supported as an import source. The importer reads labels, shapes, boundaries, and connectors from the XML and feeds them into the same validation and entity-resolution flow used for the text-based formats. Important Draw.io is a general-purpose diagram format, not a C4-specific language. The importer can extract structure from the canvas, but it cannot reliably infer all Nodinite metadata from the XML alone. Before continuing from the Draw.io XML tab, review and confirm these fields: Warning Treat Draw.io import as a structured starting point, not a lossless semantic import. Always review the suggested metadata and entity matches before Apply. Validate: Parse, Match, and Preview The Validate step parses your markup without creating any database records. It is safe to run multiple times — validate, inspect, adjust your markup, and validate again until the preview matches your expectations. Validate returns: Parsed diagram model — all nodes, boundaries, and connectors extracted from your markup Entity matches — for each node: Matched, NameConflict, or NotFound with the suggested Repository entity Mermaid preview — a rendered preview. For PlantUML and Structurizr DSL sources this shows the normalized Mermaid output the server produced, making it easy to spot translation issues before committing to Apply Parse warnings — skipped directives and unrecognized but non-fatal constructs Parse errors — fatal issues with line and column number (Mermaid parse exceptions) Review and Resolve After validation, the Review and Resolve table lists every node parsed from your markup. Rows auto-matched with high confidence are pre-filled — you confirm or override. Rows with conflicts or unresolved names require a deliberate choice. When the source is Draw.io XML, the wizard first asks you to confirm the diagram metadata. A detected name is only a suggestion; the correct Level / Diagram Type and Diagram State must be selected explicitly before you continue to entity resolution. Match Statuses Resolution Choices For each row in the table, you choose one of the following: Note External ( _Ext ) elements are automatically assigned External status. Container_Ext , ContainerDb_Ext , ContainerQueue_Ext , Component_Ext , ComponentDb_Ext , and ComponentQueue_Ext nodes appear with a grey External badge and an "Unbound placeholder" note in the Action column. No manual resolution is required, and they do not block the Apply button. These nodes are stored without a Repository binding and can be linked to an Integration or Service later in the C4 Designer. Optional overrides per row (available for any row): Target display name Component type or icon Container type and technology System type (useful for container-as-system cases) Description and C4 actor attributes Note Rows that are auto-matched and left unchanged are not sent as explicit conflict resolutions — the server applies the auto-match result directly. Only rows where you made an override or resolved a conflict are included as explicit modifications in the Apply request. Apply: Create Diagram and Build Links When you confirm the Review and Resolve table and click Import, the Apply operation creates the complete diagram in a single transaction: Creates the diagram record with the selected type, scope, and name Creates all boundary records (linked to Domains where matched, label-only otherwise) Creates all node records bound to the resolved Repository entities Creates all connector records in the original sequence from the parsed markup Creates cross-diagram links by comparing Repository bindings against all other diagrams in the same Diagram Set The newly created diagram opens immediately in Draft state. Promote it to Active when you are satisfied with the resolved entity names and the Mermaid preview. Important Step 5 — auto-link creation — only runs when the diagram belongs to a Diagram Set ( C4DiagramSetId > 0 ). Always select a set during import to get drill-through links automatically. [!TIP] Links not appearing after import? Import auto-links only evaluate diagrams present at Apply time. Use Sync Diagram Links to rebuild cross-diagram links across the full set after adding further diagrams. Practical Authoring Guidelines Include a valid diagram type declaration — every Mermaid import must begin with C4Context , C4Container , C4Component , C4Deployment , or C4Dynamic . A missing declaration causes a parse error. Keep aliases unique and stable — relationships in C4 markup are alias-based. If two nodes share an alias, relationship resolution fails. Stable aliases also make it easier to reference nodes when re-importing revised markup. For Draw.io XML — confirm the Diagram Name, Level, and Diagram State manually before continuing. If the XML contains a name, treat it as a suggestion rather than ground truth. Set C4ContainerType on Services before importing — the import wizard matches by name, but correct container types make auto-matching more confident and produce the right icons in the generated Mermaid output. Use Validate before Apply — the Validate step is free and does not write to the database. Use it to inspect match counts, review warnings, and confirm the Mermaid preview before committing. Always select a Diagram Set — Apply requires a C4DiagramSetId > 0 to be selected. Auto-link creation depends on set membership to find sibling diagrams. For PlantUML — keep to C4-PlantUML style macros and !include directives. Non-C4 PlantUML constructs outside the normalization path are not supported. For Structurizr DSL — keep to core workspace , model , views , person , softwareSystem , container , and relationship constructs. After adding or editing diagrams — run Sync Diagram Links on the Diagram Set to ensure all cross-diagram links are complete. Next Steps Sync Diagram Links – Rebuild Navigation in C4 Diagram Sets Generate from Integration Landscape – Create a Diagram Set from IL Data Creating Your First C4 Diagram Mermaid View – Read-Only Rendering Export and Sharing Repository Properties for C4 Diagramming Related Topics Architecture Diagrams Overview What is C4 Diagrams? Implementation roadmap: from first agent to platform standardization A four-phase playbook that moves your organization from a single pilot agent to enterprise-wide agentic HR in twelve months 7 min read Glossary and Reference Why phased implementation wins Enterprise software deployments have a long history of big-bang failures. Agentic HR is especially vulnerable because it requires three things to converge simultaneously: clean data, defined governance, and organizational trust. None of these can be rushed. A phased approach lets you build each layer incrementally, with proof points at every stage. This roadmap assumes you have completed the readiness assessment (see the companion article) and scored at least “conditionally ready” (60+). If your score is below 60, address the gaps identified in that assessment before entering Phase 1. Roadmap overview Phase 1: First Agent (Weeks 1-6) Objective Deploy a single agent for a single use case in a single business unit. The purpose is not scale. It is learning. You are validating that your data flows correctly, your governance model works, and your users engage with agent outputs. Selecting your first agent Choose based on three criteria: Week-by-week breakdown Phase 1 success criteria Decision gate 1 At the end of Week 6, leadership reviews Phase 1 results. Three possible outcomes: Proceed to Phase 2. Success criteria met. Expand user base and begin measuring business outcomes. Extend Phase 1. Partial success. Specific issues identified that can be resolved in 2-4 additional weeks. Pause and remediate. Fundamental gaps in data quality, governance, or user engagement that require structural investment before continuing. Phase 2: Proving (Months 2-4) Objective Expand the first agent to a broader user population (100-500 users) and shift focus from technical validation to business outcome measurement. This is where you build the ROI case that funds Phase 3. Key activities Phase 2 success criteria Decision gate 2 At Month 4, present the business case to the executive sponsor and steering committee. The gate question is: does the proven ROI justify expanding to multiple agents and business units? Phase 3: Expanding (Months 4-9) Objective Deploy additional agents (2-4), expand to multiple business units or regions, and establish the operational infrastructure for enterprise scale. Key activities Phase 3 success criteria Decision gate 3 At Month 9, the CoE reviews expansion results and recommends whether the organization is ready for full standardization. The gate question is: can the platform reliably serve all business units with consistent governance and quality? Phase 4: Standardization (Months 9-12) Objective Establish agentic HR as the default operating model across the enterprise. Shift from project mode to operational mode. Key activities Phase 4 success criteria Common pitfalls and how to avoid them Resource planning Key insight Do not skip Phase 1. The first agent is not about scale. It is about proving the data pipeline, the governance model, and the organizational willingness to trust agent outputs. Everything that follows depends on what you learn in those first six weeks. Key terms Decision gate A structured checkpoint between phases where leadership reviews results against success criteria and authorizes progression to the next phase. Minimum viable data set The smallest collection of workforce data required to activate a single agent with acceptable quality. Typically includes job titles, org structure, and basic skills data. Agent champion A business-side stakeholder (usually an HRBP or talent leader) who owns adoption, gathers feedback, and advocates for the agent within their business unit. Center of excellence (CoE) A cross-functional team responsible for agentic HR governance, best practices, and platform administration across the enterprise. Acceptance rate The percentage of agent recommendations that end users accept and act on. A leading indicator of agent quality and user trust. Crawl-walk-run A phased adoption model that starts with limited scope and gradually expands as confidence and capability grow. The bottom line Twelve months is enough time to move from zero agents to a standardized agentic HR platform, but only if you sequence correctly. Rush to Phase 3 without proving Phase 1, and you will spend more time fixing problems than delivering value. ← Previous RFP template: questions to ask every vendor Table of contents Gloat The AI-native system of action for workforce orchestration. There are two ways to run the CPU / Memory Roofline Insights perspective: from the Intel® Advisor GUI and from CLI. Intel Advisor enables you to open results collected using both methods in the GUI. Run CPU / Memory Roofline Insights Perspective from Intel® Advisor GUI In the Analysis Workflow pane, the drop-down menu to select the CPU / Memory Roofline Insights perspective, set data collection accuracy level to Low, and click the  button to run it. At this accuracy level, Intel Advisor: Measures the hardware limitations of your machine and collects loop/function timings using the Survey analysis. Collects floating-point and integer operations data, and memory data using the Characterization analysis. For details about data collection accuracy presets, see Intel Advisor User Guide: CPU Roofline Accuracy Presets. Upon completion, Intel Advisor displays a Roofline chart. The Roofline chart plots an application's achieved performance and arithmetic intensity against the machine's maximum achievable performance: Arithmetic intensity (x axis) - measured in number of floating-point operations (FLOPs) and/or integer operations (INTOPs) per byte, based on the loop/function algorithm, transferred between CPU/VPU and memory. Performance (y axis) - measured in billions of floating-point operations per second (GFLOPS) and/or billions of integer operations per second (GINTOPS). In general: Dots of different color and size represent functions/loops. The size and color of a dot represent execution time for this loop/function in relation to total execution time of the application. Large red dots are profitable to optimize as they take the longest execution time. Small green dots take less time and may be poor candidates for optimization. Diagonal lines indicate memory bandwidth limitations preventing loops/functions from achieving better performance without optimization. For example, the L1 Bandwidth roofline represents the maximum amount of work that can get done at a given arithmetic intensity if the loop always hits L1 cache. A loop does not benefit from L1 cache speed if a dataset causes it to miss L1 cache too often. In this case, it is subject to the limitations of the lower-speed L2 cache it is hitting. So, a dot representing a loop that misses L1 cache too often but hits L2 cache is positioned below the L2 Bandwidth roofline. Horizontal lines indicate compute capacity limitations preventing loops/functions from achieving better performance without optimization. For example, the Scalar Add Peak represents the peak number of add instructions that can be performed by a scalar loop under these circumstances. The Vector Add Peak represents the peak number of add instructions that can be performed under these circumstances by a vectorized loop with the highest instruction set available. So, a dot representing a loop that is not vectorized is positioned somewhere below the Scalar Add Peak roofline. A dot cannot exceed the topmost rooflines, as these represent the maximum capabilities of the machine; however, not all loops can utilize maximum machine capabilities. The greater the distance between a dot and the highest achievable roofline, the more room for optimization a function/loop has. Run CPU / Memory Roofline Insights Perspective from Command Line Interface To run CPU / Memory Roofline Insights perspective using advisor command line interface, use the following command: This command is a batch mode that runs two analyses one by one: Survey analysis that collects loops/functions execution time data. Characterization analysis that collects floating-point and integer operations, memory traffic and mask utilization metrics for AVX-512 platforms to measure arithmetic intensity and performance of your application, and compute capacity of your hardware. To view the achieved performance of your application against hardware-imposed performance ceilings on an interactive Roofline chart, open the collected results in the Intel Advisor GUI or use the following command to generate an interactive HTML Roofline report: Where report-output option specifies the directory and the HTML file into which Intel Advisor saves the generated report. For details about generating CLI reports, see the respective section in the Intel Advisor User Guide or use the following command in your terminal: Intel Advisor enables you to create a read-only result snapshot using the following command: What's Next If one or more loops is not vectorizing properly and performance is unsatisfactory: Consider working with the most time-consuming function/loop indicated on a Roofline chart. Use the Code Analytics tab to examine the main information for the selected function/loop. Refer to the Roofline pane to identify whether the function/loop is compute or memory bound. Use Recommendations tab to view hints on possible optimization steps for the selected function/loop in the Roofline Guidance section. If your loop is compute bound: Check the Vectorized Loops/Efficiency values in the Survey Report. Consider running Dependencies analysis to discover why the compiler assumed a dependency and did not vectorize the selected function/loop. Consider running Memory Access Patterns (MAP) analysis to identify expensive memory instructions. If your loop is memory bound: Examine Relationships Between Memory Levels See Also Explore the common use cases described in Intel Advisor Cookbook: Identify Bottlenecks Iteratively: Cache-Aware Roofline Optimize Memory Access Patterns using Loop Interchange and Cache Blocking Techniques Explore useful Roofline Resources for Intel Advisor Users. Intel Privacy Notice Intel Cookie Notice Strictly Necessary Cookies Strictly Necessary Cookies Always Active These technologies are necessary for the Intel experience to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. The technology is usually only set in response to actions made by the device owner which amount to a request for services, such as setting privacy preferences, logging in, filling in forms, maintaining secure login areas, maintaining state across pages (remembering items in a shopping basket), and server load balancing. The device owner can set their preference to block or alert Intel about these technologies, but some parts of the Intel experience will not work. These technologies do not store any personally identifiable information. Cookies Details Analytics Analytics [x]  Analytics Active These technologies allow Intel to count device visits and traffic sources, so Intel can measure and improve the performance of our experiences. The technology helps Intel to know which experiences are the most and least popular and see how device owners interact with the experience. All information these technologies collect is aggregated. If the device owner does not allow these technologies, then Intel will not know when the device owner visited or how the device owner interacted with our experiences. Cookies Details Functional Functional [x]  Functional Active These technologies enable the Intel experience to provide enhanced functionality and personalization. The technology may be set by Intel or by third-party providers whose services Intel have added to our experiences. If the device owner does not allow these technologies, then some or all of these services may not function properly for the device owner. Cookies Details Ad Targeting Ad Targeting and sharing choices [x]  Ad Targeting and sharing choices Active Inactive/Active Inactive means Intel will not collect and Share my device information for Ad Targeting uses. Active means Intel may collect and Share my device information for Ad Targeting uses. If you visit an Intel Site or service using a device with a United States IP address and a privacy-preference signal enabled (e.g., Do Not Track, Global Privacy Control), Intel will not collect device information for the purpose of Ad Targeting uses. If you visit an Intel Site or service using a device with a United States IP address, you have not enabled a privacy-preference signal, and your Ad Targeting and Sharing Choices are set to Active, Intel may collect device information for the purpose of Ad Targeting uses. If you have more than one device, you will need to configure all devices with your privacy-preferences. These technologies may be set through our Intel experience or by our advertising partners. The technology may be used to build a profile of the device owner's interests and show the device owner relevant advertisements on other experiences. The technology does not store directly personal information on the device, but the technology is based on uniquely identifying the device. If the device owner does not allow these technologies, then the device owner will experience less targeted advertising. Source: Human-like Content Analysis for Generative AI with Language-Grounded Sparse Encoders
Type: Pasted Text
Human-like Content Analysis for Generative AI with Language-Grounded Sparse EncodersSource: https://arxiv.org/html/2508.18236v4
Human-like Content Analysis for Generative AI with Language-Grounded Sparse Encoders Report GitHub Issue × Title: Content selection saved. Describe the issue below: Description: Submit without GitHub Submit in GitHub Back to arXiv Why HTML?
